go abroad is part of personal liberty--Whether a law which Complies with Article 21 has still to meet the challenge of Article 19-- Nature and ambit of Article 14-- Judging validity with reference to direct and inevitable effect--Whether the right under Article 19(1) (a) has any geographical limitation.
Fact	Passports Act , 1967-Ss.
Fact	3,5,6,10(3)(c), 10(5)--Whether s.10(3)(c) is violative of Articles 14, 19(1) (a) (b) & 21--Grounds for refusing to grant passport--Whether the power to impound passport arbitrary--"in general public interest" if vague.
Fact	Principles of Natural Justice--Whether applies only to quasi judicial orders or applies to administrative orders affecting rights of citizens--When statute silent whether can be implied--Duty to act judicially whether can be spelt out--In urgent cases whether principles of natural  justice can apply.
Fact	HEADNOTE: The petitioner was issued a passport on June 1, 1976 under the Passport Act , 1967.
Fact	On the 4th of July 1977, the petitioner received a letter dated 2nd July, 1977, from the Regional Passport Officer Delhi intimating to her that it was decided by <ORG> to impound her passport under s. 10(3)(c) of the Act "in public interest".
Fact	The petitioner was required to surrender her passport within 7 days from the receipt of that letter.
Fact	The petitioner immediately addressed a letter to the Regional Passport Officer requesting him to furnish a copy of the statement of reasons for making the order as provided in s.10(5).
Fact	A reply was sent by <ORG>, <ORG> on 6th July 1977 stating inter alia that the Government decided "in the interest of the general public" not to furnish her copy of the statement of reasons for the making of the order.
Fact	The petitioner thereupon filed the present Writ Petition challenging action of the Government in- impoun ding her passport and declining to give reasons for doing so.
Fact	The Act was enacted on 24-4-67 in view of the decision of this <ORG> in <NAME> case.
Fact	The position which obtained prior to the coming into force of the Act was that there was no law regulating the issue of passports for leaving the shores of India and going abroad.
Fact	The issue of passport was entirely within the unguided and unchannelled discretion of the Executive.
Fact	In <NAME> case, this <ORG> bell by a majority that the expression 'personal liberty' in Article 21 takes in, the right of locomotion and travel abroad and under Art.
Fact	21 no person can be deprived of his right to go abroad except according to the procedure established by law.
Fact	This decision was accepted by the <ORG> and the infirmity pointed but by it was set right by the enactment of the Passports Act , 1967.
Statute	The preamble of the Act shows that it was enacted to provide for the issue of passport and travel documents to regulate the departure from India of citize ns of India and other persons and for incidental and ancillary matters.
Statute	Se ction 3 provides that no person shall denart from or attempt to depart from India unless he holds in this 'behalf a valid passport or travel document.
Statute	Section 5(1) provides for making of an application for issue of a passport or travel document for visiting foreign country.
Statute	Sub-section (2) of section 5 says that on receipt of such application <ORG>, after making such enquiry, if any, as it may consider necessary, shall, by order in writing, issue or refuse to issue the passport or travel document or make or refuse to make that passport or travel document endorsement in 622 -respect of one or more of the foreign countries specified in the application.
Statute	Sub-section (3) requires <ORG> where it refuses to issue the passport or travel document or to make any endorsement to record in writing a brief statement of its reasons for making such order.
Statute	Section 6(1) lays down the grounds on which the Passport Author ity shall refuse to make an endorsement for visiting any foreign country and provides that on no other ground the endorsement shall be refused.
Statute	Section 6(2) specifies the grounds on which alone and on no other grounds <ORG> shall refuse to issue the Passport or travel document for visiting any foreign country and amongst various grounds set out there the last is that in the opinion of <ORG> the issue of passport or travel document to the applicant will not be in the public interest.
Statute	Sub-section (1) of section 10 empowers <ORG> to vary or cancel the endorsement on a passport or travel document or to vary or cancel it on the conditions subject to which a passport or travel document has been issued having regard to, inter alia, the provisions of s. 6(1) or any notification under s. 19 .
Statute	Sub-section (2) confers powers on <ORG> to vary or cancel the conditions of the passport or travel document on the application of the holder of the passport or t ravel document and with the previous approval of <ORG>.
Statute	Sub-section (3) provides that <ORG> may impound or cause to be impounded or revoke a passport or travel document on the grounds set out in cl.
Fact	(a) to (h).
Fact	The order impounding the passport in the present, case, was made by <ORG> under cl.
Fact	(c) which reads as follows :- "(c) If the passport authority deems it necessary so to do in the interest of the sovereignty and integrity of India, the security of India, friendly relations of India with the foreign country, or in the interests of the general public."
Statute	Sub-section (5) requires <ORG> impounding or revoking a passport or travel document or varying or cancelling an endorsement made upon it to record in writing a brief statement of the reasons for making such order and furnish to the holder of the passport or travel document on demand a copy of the same, unless, in any case, <ORG> is of the opinion that it will not be in the  interest of the sovereignty and integrity of India, the security of India, friendly relations of India with any foreign country, or in the interest of the general public to furnish such a copy.
Argument	<ORG> declined to furnish a copy of this statement of reasons for impounding the passport of the petitioner on the ground that it was not in the interest of the general public to furnish such copy to the petitioner.
Argument	The petitioner contended.
RatioOfTheDecision	1.The right to go abroad is part of "personal liberty" within the meaning of that expression as used in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	21 and no one can be deprived of this right except according to the procedure prescribed by law.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no procedure prescribed by the Passport Act , for impounding or revoking a Passport.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even if some procedure can be traced in the said Act it is unreasonable and arbitrary in as much as it does not provide for giving an opportunity to the holder of the Passport to be heard against the making of the order.
RulingByLowerCourt	2.
RulingByLowerCourt	Section 10(3) (c) is violative of fundamental  rights guaranteed under Articles 14,19(1) (a) and (g) and 21.
RulingByLowerCourt	3.The impugned order is made in contravention of the rules of natural justice and is, therefore, null and void.
RulingByLowerCourt	The impugned order has effect of placing an unreasonable restriction on the right of free speech and expression guaranteed to the petitioner under Article 19(1) (a) as also on the right to carry on the profession of a journalist conferred under Art.
RulingByLowerCourt	19 (1) (g).
RulingByLowerCourt	4.
RulingByLowerCourt	The impugned order could not consistently with Articles 19(1)(a) and (g)be passed on a mere information of <ORG> that the presence of the petitioner is likely to be required in connection with the proceedings before <ORG>.
RulingByLowerCourt	623 5.In order that a passport may be impounded under s. 10 (3) (c), public interest must actually exist in present and mere likelihood of public interest .arising in future would be no ground for impounding the passport.
RulingByLowerCourt	6.
Fact	It was not correct to say that the petitioner was likely to be required for .giving evidence before  <ORG>.
Fact	The respondents denied the contentions raised by the petitioner.

Argument	3.The view that Articles 19 and 21 constitute watertight compartments has been rightly over-ruled.
Argument	The doctrine that Articles 19 and 21 protect or regulate flows in different channels, was laid down in <NAME> cas e in a context which was very different from that in which that approach was displaced by the counter view that the constitution must be read as an integral whole, with possi, ble overlappings of the subject matter, of what is sought to be protected by its various provisions, particularly by articles relating to fundamental rights.
Precedent	The ob.
Precedent	servations in <NAME> case that due process with regard to law relating to preventive detention are to be found in Art.
Precedent	22 of the Constitution because it is a self-contained code for laws.
Precedent	That observation was the real ratio decidendi of <NAME>'s case.
Precedent	Other observations relating to the separability of the subject matters of Art.

Precedent	This <ORG> has already held in <NAME> case by reference to the decision from <NAME>'s cast that the ambit of personal liberty protected by Art.
Precedent	21 is wide and comprehensive.
Fact	The questions relating to either deprivation or restrictions of per sonal liberty, concerning laws falling outside Art.
Fact	22 remain really unanswered by the <NAME>'s case.
Fact	The field of 'due process' for cases of preventive detenu tion is fully covered by Art.
Fact	22 but other parts of that field not covered by Art 22 are 'unoccupied' by its specific provisions.
Fact	In what may be called unoccu -pied portions of the vast sphere of personal liberty, the substantive as well as procedural laws made to cover them must satisfy the requirements of both Arts 14 and 19 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	[646 E-H, 647 B-D, 648 A-B] Articles dealing with different fundamental rights contained in Part HI of the ,Constitution do not represent entirely separate streams of rights which do not ,mingle at many points.
RatioOfTheDecision	They are all parts of an integrated scheme in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Their waters must mix to constitute that grand flow unimpeded .and impartial justice (social, economic and political), freedom (not only of thought, expression, belief, faith and worship, but also of association, movement vocation or occupation as well as of acquisition and possession of  reasonable property), or equality (of status and of opportunity, which imply absence of unreasonable or unfair discrimination between individuals, groups and classes), and of fraternity (assuring dignity-of the individual and the unity of the nation) 624 which our Constitution visualfses.
RatioOfTheDecision	Isolation of various aspects of human freedom, for purposes of their protection, is neither realistic nor beneficial but would defeat very objects of such protection.
Precedent	[648 B-D] <NAME>'s theory of natural rights cannot be rejected as totally irfelevantIf we have advanced today towards higher civilization and in a more enlightened era we cannot lag behind what, at any rate, was the meaning given to 'personal.
Precedent	liberty' long ago by <NAME>.
Precedent	Both the rights of personal security and personal liberty recognised by what <NAME> termed 'natural law' are embodied in Act.
Precedent	21 of the Constitution.
Precedent	[649 A-C, 650 H, 651 A-B] <NAME> vs. <NAME>  Supp.
Precedent	S.C.R.
Precedent	172 relied on.
Precedent	The natural law rights were meant to be con verted into our constitutionally recognised fundamental rights so that they are to be found within it and not outside it.
Precedent	To take a contrary view would involve a conflict between natural law and our constitutional law.
RatioOfTheDecision	A 'divorce between natural law and our constitutional law would be disastrous.
RatioOfTheDecision	It would defeat one of the basic purposes of our Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	[652 B-C] The total effect and not the mere form of a restriction would determine which.
RatioOfTheDecision	fundamental right is really involved in a particular case and whether a restriction.
RatioOfTheDecision	upon its exercise is reasonably permissible on the facts and circumstances of that case.
Precedent	[652 H, 653A] If rights under Art.
Precedent	19 are rights which inhere in Indian citizens, individuals carry these inherent fundamental constitutional rights with them-wherever they go, in so far as our law applies to them, because they are part of the Indian National just as Indian ships, flying the Indian flag are deemed in international law to be floating parts of Indian territory.
Precedent	This analogy, however , could not be pushed too far because Indian citizens, on foreign territory, are only entitled by virtue of their Indian Nationality and Passports to the protection of the Indian Republic and the assistance of its Diplomatic Missions abroad.
Precedent	They cannot claim to be governed abroad by their own constitutional or personal laws which do not operate outside India.
Precedent	[653 A-C] In order to apply the test contained in Arts.
Precedent	14 and 19 of the Constitution we have to consider the objects for which the exercise of inherent rights recognised by Art.
Precedent	21 of the Constitution are restricted as well as the procedure by which these restrictions are sought to be imposed, both substantive and procedural laws and actions taken under them will have to pass the test imposed by, Arts.
Precedent	14 and 19, whenever facts justifying the invocation of either of these Articles may be disclosed, for example, an international singer or dancer may well be able to complain of an unjustifiable restriction on professional activity by denial of a passport .
Precedent	In such a case, violation of both Arts.
Precedent	21 and 19(1)(g) may be put forward making it necessary for the authorities concerned to justify the restriction imposed by showing satisfaction of tests of validity contemplated by each of 'these two Articles.
Precedent	[653 F-H] The tests of reason and justice cannot be abstract.
Precedent	They cannot be divorced' from the needs of the nation.
Precedent	The tests have to be pragmatic otherwise they would cease to be reasonable.
Precedent	The discretion left to the authority to impound a passport in Public interest cannot invalidate the law itself.
Precedent	We cannot, out of fear, that such power will be misused, refuse to permit <ORG> to entrust even such power to executive authorities as may be absolutely necessary to carry out the purposes of a validly exercisable power.
Precedent	In matters such as, grant, suspension, impounding or cancellation of passports, the possible dealing of an individual with nationals and authorities of other States have to be considered.
Precedent	The contemplated or possible activities abroad of the  individual may have to be taken into account.
Precedent	There may be questions of national safety and welfare which transcend the importance of the individual's inherent right to go where he or she pleases to go.
Precedent	Therefore, the grant of wide discretionary power to the exe- cutive authorities cannot be considered as unreasonable yet there must be procedural safeguards to ensure that the Power will not be used for purposes extraneous to the grant of the power.
Precedent	The procedural proprieties must be insisted upon.
Precedent	[654 A-E] 625 A bare, look at the provisions.
Precedent	of s. 10(3) shows.
Precedent	that each of the orders which- could be passed; under s. 10(3) (a) and (b) requires a satisfaction of <ORG> on certain objective conditions which must exist in a case before it passes an order to impound a passport or a travel document.
Precedent	Impounding or revocation are placed side by side on the same footing in the provisions [654 G-H] It is clear from the provisions of the Act that there is a statutory right also acquired, on fulfilment  of the prescribed conditions by the holder of a passport, that it should continue to be effective for the specified period so long as no ground has come into existence for either its revocation or for impounding it which amounts to a suspension of, it for the time being.
Precedent	It is true that in a proceedings.
Precedent	under Art.
Precedent	32 , the <ORG> is concerned only with the, enforcement of fundamental constitutional rights and not with any statutory rights apart from fundamental.
Precedent	rights.
Precedent	Article 21 , however, makes it Clear that violation of all law whether statutory or of any other kind is itself an infringement of the guaranteed fundamental right.
Precedent	[655 B- D] The orders under s. 10(3) must be based upon some material even if that material concerns in some cases of reasonable suspicion arising from certain credible assertions made by reliable individuals.
Precedent	In an emergent situation, the impounding of a passport may become necessary without even giving an opportunity to be heard against such a step which could be reversed after a n opportunity is given to the holder of the passport to show why the step was unnecessary.
Precedent	However, ordinarily no passport could be reasonably either impounded or revoked without giving a prior opportunity to its holder to show cause against the proposed action.
Precedent	[655 D-E] It is well-settled that even when there is no specific provision in a statute or rules made thereunder for showing case against action proposed to be taken against an individual, which affects the right of that individual the duty to give reasonable opportunity to be heard will be implied from the nature of the function to be perform,,,' by the authority which has the power to take punitive or damaging action.
Precedent	[655 <CITATION_1455346> .
Precedent	AIR  SC 1269 @ 1271 relied on.
Precedent	<NAME> v. <ORG>,  14 C.B.
Precedent	(N. S.) 180 quoted with approval.
Precedent	An order impounding a passport must be made quasi- judicially.
Precedent	This was not done in the present case.
RatioOfTheDecision	It cannot be said that a good enough reason has been shown too exist for impounding the passport o f the petitioner.
RatioOfTheDecision	The petitioner had no opportunity of showing that the ground for impounding it given in this Court either does not exist or has no bearing on public interest or that the public in- terest can be better served in some other manner.
RatioOfTheDecision	The order should be quashed and the respondent should be directed to give an opportunity to the petitioner to show cause against any proposed action on such grounds as may be available.
RatioOfTheDecision	[656 E-G] There were no pressing grounds with regard to the petitioner that the immediate action of impounding her passport was called for.
RatioOfTheDecision	The rather cavalier fashion in which the disclosure of any reason for impounding of her passport was denied to the petitioner despite the fact that the only reason said to exist is the possibility of her being called to give evidence before <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a ground is not such as to be reasonably deemed to necessitate its concealment in public interest.
RatioOfTheDecision	[656 G-H] Even executive authorities when taking administrative action which in volves any deprivation of or restriction on inherent fundamental rights of citizens must take care to see that justice is not only done but manifestly appears to be done.
RatioOfTheDecision	They have a duty to proceed in a way which is free from even the appearance of arbitrariness, unreasonableness or unfairness.
RatioOfTheDecision	They have to act in a manner which is patently impartial and meets the requirements of natural justice [657 A-B] 62 6 As the undertaking given by the Attorney General amounts to an offer to deal with the petitioner justly and fairly after informing her of any ground that may exist for impounding her passport, no further action by this Court is necessary.
Precedent	[657 C-D] The impugned order must be quashed and Passport Authorities be directed to return the passport to the petitioner.
Precedent	Petition allowed with costs.
Precedent	[657D] <NAME>, J.
Precedent	(concurring with <NAME>, J.)
RatioOfTheDecision	The power to refuse to disclose the reasons for impounding a passport is of an exceptional nature and it ought to be exercised fairly, sparingly and only when full y justified by the exigencies of an uncommon situation.
RatioOfTheDecision	The reasons if disclosed, being open to judicial scrutiny for ascertaining their nexus with the order impounding the passport, the refusal to disclose the reasons would also be open to the scrutiny of the court; or else the wholesome power of a dispassionate judicial examination of executive orders could with impunity be set at nought by an obdurate determination to suppress the reasons.
RatioOfTheDecision	The disclosure made under the stress of the Writ Petition that the petitioner's passport was impounded because, her presence was likely to be required in connection with the proceedings before <ORG>, could easily have been made when the petitioner called upon the Government to let her know the reasons why her passport was impounded.
Precedent	[658 A-D] In <NAME> case this <ORG> ruled, by majority, that the expression personal liberty which occurs in Art.
Precedent	21 of the Constitution includes the right to travel abroad and that no person can be deprived  of that right except according to procedure established by law.
Precedent	The mere prescription of some kind of procedure cannot even meet the mandate of Article 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	The procedure prescribed by law has to be fair, just and reasonable, not fanciful, oppressive or arbitrary.
RatioOfTheDecision	The question whether the procedure prescribed by law which curtails or takes away the personal liberty guaranteed by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	21 is reasonable or not has to be considered not in the abstract or on hypothetical considerations like the provision for a full-dressed hearing as in a court room trial but in the contest, primarily, of the purpose which the Act is intended to achieve and of urgent situations which those who are charged with the duty of administering the Act may be called upon to deal with.
RatioOfTheDecision	Secondly, even the fullest compliance with the requirements of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	21 is not the journey's end because a law which prescribes fair and reasonable procedure for curtailing or taking away the personal liberty granted by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	21 has still to meet a possible cha llenge under the other provi- sions of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the Bank Nationalisation case the majority held that the assumption in <NAME> case that certain Articles of the Constitution exclusively deal with specific matters cannot be accepted as correct.
RatioOfTheDecision	Though the Bank Nationalisation case was concerned with the inter-relationship of Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	31 and 19 and not of Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	21 and 19, the basic approach adopted therein as regards the construction of fundamental rights guaranteed in the different provisions of the Constitution categorically discarded the major premise of the majority judgment in <NAME>'s case.
RatioOfTheDecision	[658 D-G, 659 A-B] The test of directness of the impugned law as contrasted with its consequence was thought in A. K. <NAME> and <NAME>'s case to be the true approach for determining whether a fundamental right was infringed.
RatioOfTheDecision	A significant application of that test may be perceived in <NAME> case where an order passed by <ORG> prohibiting the publication of a witness's  evidence in a defamation case was upheld by this <ORG> on the ground that it was passed with the object of affording protection to the witness in order to obtain true evidence and its impact on the right of free speech and expression guaranteed by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19 (1) (a) was incidental.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> in <ORG> struck a modified note by evolving the test of proximate effect and operation of the Statute.
Precedent	That test saw its fruition in <ORG> case where the <ORG> giving precedence to the direct and immediate effect of the order over the form and object, struck down the Daily Newspapers (Price and Page) Order, 1960, on the ground that it violated Article 19(1)(a) of the Constitution.
Precedent	The culmi- 627 nation of this thought process was reached in the Bank Nationalisation case where it was held by the majority, speaking through <ORG>, that the extent of protection against the impairment of a fundamental right is determined by the direct operation of an action upon the individual's rights and not  by the object of the <ORG> or by the form of the action.
Precedent	In <NAME> case the <ORG> reiterated the same position.
Precedent	It struck down the newsprint policy restricting the number of pages of newspapers without the option to reduce the circulation as offending against the provisions of Art.
Precedent	19(1)(a).
Precedent	[659F-H, 660 A-C] Article 19(1)(a) guarantees to Indian Citizens the right to freedom of speech and expression.
Precedent	It does not delimit the grant of that right in any manner and there is no reason arising either out of interpretational dogmas or pragmatic considerations why courts should strain the language of the Article to cut down amplitude of that right.
Precedent	The plain meaning of the clause guaranteeing free speech and expression is that Indian citizens are entitled to exercise that right wherever they choose regardless of geographical considerations.
Precedent	[661 A-D] The Constitution does not confer any power on the executive to prevent the exercise by an Indian citizen of the right of free speech and expression on  foreign soil.
Precedent	The Constitution guarantees certain fundamental freedoms except where their exercise is limited by territorial considerations.
Precedent	Those freedoms may be exercised wheresoever one chooses subject to the exceptions or qualifications mentioned in Art.
Precedent	19 itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	The right to go out of India is not an integral part of the right of free speech and expression.
RatioOfTheDecision	The analogy of the freedom of press being included in the right of free speech and expression is wholly misplaced because the right of free expression incontrovertibly includes the right of freedom of press.
RatioOfTheDecision	The right to go abroad on one hand and the right of free speech and expression on the other are made up basically of constituents so different that one cannot be comprehended in the other.
RatioOfTheDecision	The presence of the due process clause in the 5th and 14th amendments of the American Constitution makes significant difference to the approach of American Judges to the definition and evaluation of constitutional guarantees.
Precedent	This Court rejected the contention  that the freedom.
Precedent	to form associations or unions contained in Article 19(1)(c) carried with it the right that a workers , union could do all that was necessary to make that right effective in order to achieve the purpose for which the union was formed.
Precedent	[See the decision in <ORG>.
Precedent	[661 F, H, 662 A-13, E] <NAME>, J.
Precedent	(for himself <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ) The fundamental rights in Part III of the Constitution represent the basic values cherished by the people of this country since the Vedic times and they are calculated to protect the dignity of the individual and create conditions in which every human being can develop his personality to the fullest extent.
Precedent	But these freedoms are not and cannot be absolute, for absolute and unrestricted freedom of one may be destructive of the freedom of another.
Precedent	In a well ordered civilised society, freedom can only be regulated freedom.
Precedent	It is obvious that Article 21 though couched in negative language confers fundamental right to lif e and personal liberty.
Precedent	The question that arises for consideration on the language of Art.
Precedent	21 is as to what is the meaning and content of the words .personal liberty' as used in this Article.
Precedent	In <NAME> case a narrow interpretation was placed on the words 'personal liberty.'
Precedent	But there was no definite pronouncement made on this point since the question before the court was not so much the interpretation of the words 'personal liberty' as the inter- relation between Arts.
Precedent	19 and 21.
Precedent	[667 G-H, 668 D-E, G, H, 669 <CITATION_1857950>  SCR 88 and <CITATION_619152> .
Precedent	1 SCR 332 referred to.
Precedent	In <NAME> case the majority of this Court held that 'personal liberty' is used in the Article as a compendious term to include within itself all varieties of Tights which go to make up the personal liberties of man other than those dealt with in several clauses of Article 19(1).
Precedent	The minority however took the view that the expression personal liberty is a comprehensive one and the right to move freely is an attribut e of personal liberty.
Precedent	The minority observed that it was not right to exclude any attribute of personal liberty from the scope 628 and ambit of Art.
Precedent	21 on the ground that it was covered by Art.
Precedent	19(1) It was pointed out by the, minority that both Articles 19(1)and 21 are independent fundamental rights though there is a certain amountof overlapping; and there is no question of one being carved out of another.
Precedent	The minority view was upheld as correct and it was pointed out that it wouldnot be tight to read the expression 'personal liberty' in Art.
Precedent	21 in a narrowand restricted sense so as to exclude those attributes of personal liberty which are specifically dealt with in Art.
Precedent	19(1).

RatioOfTheDecision	Article 14 is a founding faith of the Constitution.

Precedent	[673 H, 674 <CITATION_1327287>  2 SCR 348 applied.
Precedent	Equality and arbitrariness are sworn enemies; one belongs to the rule of law in a republic while the other to the whim and caprice of an absolute monarch.
Precedent	Article 14 strikes at arbitrariness in <ORG> action and ensures fairness and equality ,of treatment.
Precedent	The principle of reasonableness which legally as well as philosophically, is an essential element of equality or non-arbitrariness pervades Article 14 like a brooding omni-presence and the procedure contemplated by Article 21 must answer the test of reasonableness in order to be in conformity with Article 14.
Precedent	It must be right and just and fair and not arbitrary, fanciful or oppressive.
Precedent	[674 B-C] It is true that the Passports Act does not provide for giving reasonable opportunity to the holder of the passport to be heard in advance before impounding a passport.
Precedent	But that is not conclusive of the question.
Precedent	If the statute make  itself clear onthis point, then no more question arises but even when statute is silent the lawmay in a given case make an implication and apply the principle.
Precedent	Naturaljustice is a great humanising principle intended to invest law with fairness and to secure justice and over the years it has grown into a widely pervasive rule affecting large areas of administrative action.
Precedent	[674 F-G, 675 <CITATION_1004041>  A.C. 297 approved.
Precedent	<NAME> v. Secretary of State for Home Affairs  112 Solicitor General 690 approved.
Precedent	There can be no distinction between a quasi-judicial function and an administrative function for the purpose of principles of natural justice.
Precedent	The aim of both administrative inquiry as well as the quasi-judicial enquiry is to arrive at a just decision and if a rule of natural justice is calculated to secure justice or to put it 'negatively, to prevent miscarriage of justice, it is difficult to see why it should be applicable to quasi- judicial enquiry and not to administrative enquiry.
Precedent	It must logically app ly to both.
Precedent	It cannot be said that the requirements of fairplay in action is any the less in an administrative enquiry than in a quasi-judicial one.
Precedent	Sometimes an unjust decision in an administrative enquiry may have far more serious consequences than a decision in a quasi-judicial enquiry and hence rules of natural justice must apply, equally in an administrative enquiry which entails civil consequences.

Precedent	<CITATION_485924>  3 S.C.C.
Precedent	864 relied on 630 The law is not well settled that even in an administrative proceeding which involves civil consequences the doctrine of natural justice must be held to be applicable.
Precedent	[680 A] The power conferred on <ORG> is to impound a passport and the consequence of impounding a passport would be to impair the constitutional right of the holder of the passport to go abroad during the time that the passport is impounded.
Precedent	The passport can be impounded only on certain specified grounds set out in section 10(3) and <ORG> would have to apply its mind to the facts and circumstances of a given case and decide whether any of the specified grounds exists which would Justify impounding of the passport.
Precedent	The authority is also required by s. 10(5) to record in writing a brie f statement of the reasons for making the order impounding a passport and save in certain exceptional situations, the authority is obliged to' furnish a copy of the statement of reasons to the holder of the passport.
Precedent	Where <ORG> which has impounded a passport is other than <ORG> a right of appeal against the order impounding the passport is given by section 11 .
Precedent	Thus, the power conferred on <ORG> to impound a passport is a quasijudicial power.
Precedent	The rules of natural justice would in the circumstances be applicable in the exercise of the power of impounding a passport even on the orthodox view which prevailed prior to <NAME> case.
Precedent	The same result must follow in view of the decision in <NAME> case, even if the power to impound a passport were regarded as administrative in character, because it seriously interferes with the constitutional right of the holder of the passport to go abroad and entails adverse civil consequences.
Precedent	The argument of the  Attorney General however was that having regard to the nature of the action involved in the impounding of a passport, the <ORG> must be held to be excluded because if notice were to be given to the holder of the passport and reasonable opportunity afforded to him to show cause why his passport should not be impounded he might immediately on the strength of the passport make good his exit from the country and the object of impounding etc., would be frustrated.
RatioOfTheDecision	Now it is true that there may be cases where, having regard to the nature of the action to be taken, its object and purpose and the scheme of the relevant statutory provision, fairness in action may warrant exclusion of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Indeed, there are certain wellrecognised exceptions to the audi alteram Partem rule established by judicial decisions.
RatioOfTheDecision	These exceptions, do not in any way militate against the principle which requires fair play in administrative action.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word exception is really a misnomer because in the se exceptional cases the audi alteram partem rule is held inapplicable not by way of an exception to fairplay in action but because nothing unfair can be inferred by not conferring an opportunity to present or meet a case.
RatioOfTheDecision	The life of the law is not logic but experience.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, every legal proposition must in the ultimate analysis be tested on Me touch-stone of pragmatic realism.
RatioOfTheDecision	[680 B-F, H, 681 C-F] The audi alteram partem rule may, therefore, by the experiential test, be excluded, if importing the right to be heard has the effect of paralysing the administrative process or the need for promptitude or the urgency of the situation so demands.
RatioOfTheDecision	But, at the same time, it must be remembered that this is a rule of vital importance in the field of administrative law and it must not be jettisoned save in very exceptional circumstances where Compulsive necessity so demands.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is a wholesome rule designed to secure the rule of law and the <ORG> should not be too ready to eschew it in its application to a given c ase.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> must make every effort to salvage this cardinal rule to the maximum extent permissible in a given case.
RatioOfTheDecision	The audi alteram partem rule is not cast in a rigid mould and judicial decisions establish that it may stiffer situational modifications.
RatioOfTheDecision	The core of it must, however, remain, namely, that the person affected must have reasonable opportunity' of being heard and the hearing must be a genuine hearing and not an empty public relations exercise.
RatioOfTheDecision	It would, not therefore be right to conclude that the audi alteram partem?
Argument	rule is excluded merely because the power to impound a passport might be frustrated, if prior notice and hearing were to be given to the person concerned before impounding his passport.
Argument	<ORG> may proceed to impound the passport without giving any prior opportunity to the person concerned to be heard, but as soon as the order impounding 631 the Passport is made, an opportunity of hearing, remedial in aim, should be given to him so that he may present his case and co ntrovert that of <ORG> and point out why his passport should not be impounded and the order impounding it recalled.
Argument	This should not only be possible but also quite appropriate, because the reasons for impounding the passport are required to be supplied by <ORG> after the making of the order and the person affected would, therefore, be in a position to make a representation setting forth his case and plead for setting aside the action impounding his passport.
Argument	A fair opportunity of being heard following immediately upon the order impounding the Passport would satisfy the mandate of natural justice and a provision requiring giving of such opportunity to the person concerned can and should be read by implication in the Passports Act .
Argument	If such a provision were held to be incorporated in the Act by necessary implication the procedure prescribed by the Act for impounding a passport would be right, fair and just and would not suffer from arbitrainess or unreasonableness.
Argument	Therefore, t he procedure established by the Passport Act for impounding a passport must be held to be in conformity with the requirement of Art.
Argument	21 and does not fall foul of that Article.
Argument	[681 G-H, 682 A-C, E-H, 683 A-B] In the present case, however, <ORG> not only did not give an opportunity of hearing of the petitioner after making the impugned order impounding her passport but even declined to furnish to the petitioner the reasons for impounding her passport despite requests made by her.
Argument	<ORG> was wholly unjustified in withholding the reasons for impounding the passport and this was not only in breach of the statutory provisions but it also amounted to denial of opportunity of hearing to the petitioner.
Argument	The order impounding the passport of the petitioner was, therefore, clearly in violation of the rule of natural justice embodied in the maxim audi alteram partem and was not in conformity with the procedure prescribed by the Act.
Argument	The learned Attorney General, however, made a statement  on behalf of <ORG> that the Government was agreeable to considering any representation that may be made by the petitioner in respect of the impounding of her passport and giving her an opportunity in the matter, and that the representation would be dealt with expeditiously in accordance with law.
RatioOfTheDecision	This statement removes the vice from the order impounding the passport and it can no longer be assailed on the ground that it does not comply with the audi alteram partem rule or is not in accord with the procedure prescribed by the Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	[683 C-G] The law is well settled that when a statute vests unguided and unrestricted power in an authority to affect the rights of a person without laying down any policy or principle which is to guide the authority,, in exercise of the power, it would be affected by the vice of discrimination since it would leave it open to the authority to discriminate between persons and things similarly situated.
RatioOfTheDecision	However, it is difficult to say that the discretion conferred on  <ORG> is arbitrary or unfettered.
RatioOfTheDecision	There are four grounds set out in section 10(3)(c) which would justify the making of an order impounding a passport.
RatioOfTheDecision	[684C-D] The words "in the interest of the general public" cannot be characterised as vague or undefined.
RatioOfTheDecision	The expression "in the interest of the general public" has clearly a well defined meaning and the <ORG> have often been called upon to decide whether a particular action is in the interest of general public or in public interest and no difficulty has been experienced by the <ORG> in carrying out this exercise.
RatioOfTheDecision	These words are in fact borrowed ipsissima verba from Art 19(5) and it would be nothing short of heresay to accuse the constitution makers of vague and loose thinking.
RatioOfTheDecision	Sufficient guidelines are provided by the Act itself and the power conferred on <ORG> to impound a passport cannot be said to be unguided or unfettered.
RatioOfTheDecision	Moreover the exercise of this power is not made dependent on the subjective opinion of the <ORG> t Authority as regards the necessity of exercising it on one or more grounds stated in S.10(3)(c), but <ORG> is required to record in writing a brief statement of reasons for impounding the passport and save in certain exceptional circumstances, supply a copy of such statement of reasons to the person affected so that the person concerned can challenge the decision of <ORG> in appeal and <ORG> can examine whether the reasons given by the Passport Autho- 632 rity are correct and if so whether they justify the making of the order impounding the passport.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is true that when the order impounding the passport is made by <ORG> there is no appeal against it.
RatioOfTheDecision	But it must be remembered that in such a case the power is exercised by <ORG> itself and it can safely be assumed that <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	will exercise the power in a reasonable and responsible manner.
RatioOfTheDecision	When power is vested in a high authority like <ORG> abus e of power cannot be lightly assumed and in any event, if there is abuse if the power the arms of the <ORG> are long enough to reach it and to strike it down.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power conferred on <ORG> to impound a passport under section 10(3) (c) cannot be regarded as discriminatory.
Precedent	[684-D-H, 685 A-C] The law on the point viz.
Precedent	the proper test or yard-stick to be applied for determining whether a statute infringes a particular fundamental right, while adjudging the constitutionality of a statute on the touchstone of fundamental rights has undergone radical changes since the days of <NAME> case  SCR 88, which was followed in <CITATION_40472258>  SCR 451 and applied in <CITATION_1643138> .
Precedent	3 SCR 744, [685 D-G, 686-B] According to these decisions, the theory was that the object and form of state action determine the extent of protection which may be claimed by an individual and the validity of such action has to be judged by considering whether it is "directly in respect of the subject covered by an y particular article of the Constitution or touches the said article only incidentally or indirectly".
Precedent	The test to be applied for determining the constitutional validity of state action with fundamental right therefore was : what is the object of the authority in taking the action : What is the subject matter of the action and to which fundamental right does it relate ?
Precedent	This theory that "the extent of protection of important guarantees, such as the liberty of persons and right to property, depend upon the form and object of the state action not upon its direct operation upon the individual's freedom" held sway, in spite of three decisions of <ORG> in <CITATION_1880952> .
Precedent	SCR 674; <ORG> JUDGMENT:  <CITATION_243002>  3 SCR 842 formulating the test of direct and inevitable effect or the doctrine of intended and real effect for the purpose of adjudging whether a statute offends a particular fundamental right.
Precedent	However, it was only in R.C.
Precedent	Cooper v. Union of India  3 SCR 530 that t he doctrine that the object and form of the <ORG> action alone determine the extent of protection that may be claimed by an individual and that the effect of the <ORG> action on the fundamental right of the individual is irrelevant as laid down in <NAME>'s case was finally rejected.
RatioOfTheDecision	This doctrine is in substance and reality nothing else than the test of pith and substance which is applied for determining the constitutionality of legislation where there is conflict of legislative powers conferred on <ORG> and <ORG> legislatures with reference to legislative lists.
RatioOfTheDecision	[685 H, 686 A-B, D-H, 687 A-E, F-G] The test applied since <NAME> case was as to what is the direct and inevitable consequence or effect of the impugned state action on the fundamental right of the petitioner.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is possible that in a given case the pith and substance of the <ORG> action may deal with a particular fundamental right but its direct and inevitable effect may be on another fundamental right and in that case, the state action wo uld have to meet the challenge of the latter fundamental right.
RatioOfTheDecision	The pith and substance doctrine looks only at the object and subject matter of the state action, but in testing the validity of the state action with reference to fundamental rights, what the <ORG> must consider is the direct and inevitable consequence of the <ORG> action.
RatioOfTheDecision	Otherwise the protection of the fundamental rights would subtly but surely eroded.
Precedent	[690 <CITATION_1857950>  2 SCR 88; <CITATION_40472258>  SCR 451; <CITATION_1643138> .
Precedent	3 SCR 744 referred to.
Precedent	<NAME> v. <ORG>  3 SCR 530; <CITATION_1880952> .
Precedent	SCR 674; <CITATION_1157189> ,  S.C.R.
Precedent	12 and <CITATION_243002>  3 SCR 842; quoted with approval, <CITATION_125596>  2 SCR 757 applied.
Precedent	The test formulated in <NAME>'s case merely refers to "direct operation" or "direct consequence and effect" of the <ORG> action on the fundamental right of the petitioner and does not use the word "inevitable" in this connection.
Precedent	If the test were merely of direct or indirect effect, it  would be an open-ended concept and in the absence of operational criteria for judging "directness" it would give the Court an unquestionable discretion to decide whether in a given case a consequence or effect is direct or not.
Precedent	Some other concept-vehicle would be needed to quantify the extent of directness or indirectness in order to apply the test.
Precedent	And that is supplied by the criterion of "inevitable" consequence or effect adumbrated in the <ORG> case (1959) SCR 12.
Precedent	This criterion helps to quantify the extent of directness necessary to constitute infringement of a fundamental right.
Precedent	Now, if the effect of <ORG> action on a fundamental right is direct and inevitable, then a fortiorari it must be presumed to have been intended by the authority taking the action and hence this doctrine of direct and inevitable effect is described aptly as the doctrine of intended and real effect.
Precedent	This is the test which must be applied for the purpose of determining whether section 10(3)(c) , or the impugned order ma de under it is violated of Art.
Precedent	19(1)(a) or (g).
Precedent	[698 C-F] Prima facie, the right which is sought to be restricted by s. 10(3)(c) and the impugned order is the right to go abroad and that is not named as a fundamental right or included in so many words in Art.
Precedent	19(1)(a) of the Constitution.
Precedent	The right to go abroad, as held in <NAME> case  3 SCR 525, is included in "personal liberty" within the meaning of Art.
Precedent	21 and is thus a fundamental right protected by that Article.
Precedent	This clearly shows that there is no underlying principle in the Constitution which, limits the fundamental right in their operation to the territory of India.
Precedent	If a fundamental right under Art.
Precedent	21 can be exercisable outside India, there is no reason why freedom of speech and expression conferred under 19(1)(a) cannot be so exercisable.
Precedent	[690 H, 694 <CITATION_1747577> .
Precedent	Possport Officer, <ORG>, New Delhi & Ors.,  3 SCR 525; <NAME> v. United States, 184 Federal Reporter (ed) p 131, referred to.
Precedent	<CITATION_1274822>  Mysore Law  Journal p. 605 approved.
Precedent	There are no geographical limitations to freedom of speech and expression guaranteed under Art.
Precedent	19(1) (a) and this freedom is exercisable not only in India but also outside and if State action sets up barriers to its citizens' freedom of expression in any country in the world, it would violate Art.
Precedent	19(1) (a) as much as if it inhibited such expression within the country.
Precedent	This conclusion would on a parity of reasoning apply equally in relation to fundamental right to practise any profession or to carry on any occupation, trade or business, guaranteed under Art.
Precedent	19(1)(g).
Precedent	[694G-H, 695 A] Freedom to go abroad incorporates the important function of an ultimum refunium liberatis when other basic freedoms are refused.
Precedent	Freedom to go abroad has much social value and represents a basic human right of great significance.
Precedent	It is in fact incorporated as in alienable human right in Article 13 of the Universal Declaration of Human Rights.
Precedent	But it is not specifically named as a fundamental right in Art .
Precedent	19(1) of the Constitution.
Precedent	[696 C-D] <NAME> v. <NAME>, 357 US 116 : 2 L.ed 2d, 1204 referred to.
Precedent	Even if a right is not specifically named in Art.
Precedent	19(1) it may still be a fundamental right covered by some clause of that Article, if it is an integral part of a named fundamental right or partakes of the same basic nature and character as that fundamental right.
Precedent	It is not enough that a right claimed by the petitioner flows or emanates from a named fundamental right or that its existence, is necessary in order to make the exercise of the named fundamental right meaningful and effective.
Precedent	Every activity which facilitates the exercise of a named fundamental right is not necessarily comprehended in that fundamental right, nor can it be regarded as such merely because it may not be possible otherwise to effectively exercise that fundamental right.
Precedent	634  What is necessary to be seem is and that is the test which must be applied, whether the right claimed by the petitioner is an, integral part of a named fundamental rig ht or partakes of the same basic nature and character as the named fundamental, right is in reality and substance nothing but an instance of the exercise of, the named fundamental right.
Precedent	If this be the correct test, the right to go abroad cannot in all circumstances be regarded as included in freedom of speech and expression.
Precedent	[697 D-G] <NAME> v. <NAME>, 357 US.
Precedent	116.
Precedent	2 L.ed 2d.
Precedent	1204 : <CITATION_891756> .
Precedent	SCR 12; <CITATION_243002>  3 SCR 842; <CITATION_125596>  2 SCR 757; <CITATION_456839>  SCR 594 referred to.
Precedent	<NAME> v. Secretary of <ORG> 378 US 500 : 12 L.ed 2d 992; <NAME> v. Rusk 381 USI : 14 L.ed 2d 179 explained.
Precedent	The theory that a peripheral or concomitant right which facilitates the exercise of a named fundamental right or gives its meaning and substance or makes its exercise effective, is itself a guaranteed right included within the named fundamental right cannot be accepted.
Precedent	[701 <CITATION_1781810>  3 SCR 269 applied.
Precedent	The right to go abroad cannot therefore be regarded as included in freedom of speec h and expression guaranteed under Art.
Precedent	19(1)(a) on the theory of peripheral or concomitant right.
Precedent	The right to go abroad cannot be treated as part of the right to carry on trade, business or profession or calling guaranteed under Art.
Precedent	19(1)(g).
Precedent	The right to go abroad is clearly not a guaranteed right under any clause of Article 19(1) and Section 10(3) (c) which authorises imposition of restrictions on the right to go abroad by impounding of passport cannot be held as void as offending Article 19(1)(a) or (g), as its direct and inevitable impact is on the right to go abroad and not on the right of free speech and expression or the right to carry on trade, business, profession or calling.
Precedent	[702 C-E] But that does not mean that an order made under s. 10 (3) (c) may not violate Article 19(1)(a) or (g).
Precedent	Where a statutory provision empowering an authority to take action is constitutionally valid, action taken under it may offend a fundamental right and in that event, though the statutory provision is valid, the acti on may be void.
Precedent	Therefore, even though section 10(3)(c) is valid, the question would always remain whether an order made under it invalid as contravening a fundamental right.
Precedent	The direct and inevitable effect of an order impounding a passport may, in a given case, be to abridge or take away freedom of speech and expression or the right to carry on a profession and where such is the case, the order would be invalid, unless saved by Article 19(2) or Article 19(6).
Precedent	[702F- <CITATION_1276331> .,  2 SCR 375 referred to.
Precedent	Though the impugned order may be within the terms of s. 10(3) (c), it must nevertheless not contravene any fundamental right and if it does, it would be void.
Precedent	Now, even if an order impounding a passport is made in the interests of public order decency or morality, the restriction imposed by it may be so wide, excessive disproportionate to the mischief or evil sought to be averted that it may be considered unreasonable and in that event, if the direct and inevitable consequence of the order is to abr idge or take away freedom of speech and expression, it would be violative of Article 19(1)(a) and would not be protected by Article 19(2) and the same would, be the position where the order is in the interests of the general public but it infringes directly and inevitably on the freedom to carry on a profession in which case it would contravene Article 19(1) (g) without being saved by the provision enacted ion Article 19(6).
Fact	[705 D-E] 6 3 5 The impugned order, in the present case does riot Violate either Art.
Fact	19(1)(a) or Art.
Fact	19(1)(g).
Fact	What the impugned order does is to impound the passport of the petitioner and thereby prevent her from going abroad and at the date, when impugned order was made, there is nothing to show that the petitioner was intending to go abroad for the purpose of exercising her freedom or speech and expression or her right to carry on her profession as a journalist.
RatioOfTheDecision	The direct and inevitable consequence of the impugned order was to impede the exercise of her right to go abroad and not to  interfere with her freedom of speech and expression or her right to carry on her profession.
RatioOfTheDecision	[706 F-G] The petitioner is not justified in seeking to limit the expression "interests of the general public" to matters relating to foreign affairs.
RatioOfTheDecision	The argument that the said expression could not cover a situation where the presence of a person is required to give evidence before a commission of Inquiry_ is plainly erroneous as it seeks to cut down the width and amplitude of the expression "interests of the general public," an expression which has a well recognised legal connotation and which is found in Article 19(5) as well as Article 19(6).
RatioOfTheDecision	It is true that that there is always a perspective within which a statute is intended to operate, but that does not justify reading of a statutory provision in a manner not warranted by the language or narrowing down its scope and meaning by introducing a limitation which has no basis either in the, language or in the context of a statutory provision Clauses (d), (e) and (h)  of S. 10(3) make it clear that there are several grounds in this section which do not relate to foreign affairs.
RatioOfTheDecision	[709 B-F] Moreover the present case is not one where the maxim "expressio unius exclusio ulterius has any application at all.
RatioOfTheDecision	[710-B-C] <ORG> v. <NAME> & Anr.,  3 SCR 108 @ 128 referred to.
RatioOfTheDecision	OBSERVATION It is true that the power under s. 10(3) (c) is rather a drastic power to interfere with a basic human right, but this power has been conferred by the legislature in public interest and there is no doubt that it will be sparingly used and that too, with great care and circumspection and as far as possible.
RulingByLowerCourt	the passport of a person will not be impounded merely on the ground of his being required in connection with a proceeding, unless the case is brought within s. 10(3)(e) or sec.
RulingByLowerCourt	10(3)(b).
RulingByLowerCourt	[710G-H] <NAME> v. <NAME>  I Q.
Statute	B 693 quoted with approval.
RatioOfTheDecision	An order impounding a passport can be made by <ORG> only if it is actually in the interests of the general public  to do so and it is not enough that the interests of the general public may be likely to be served in future by the making of the order.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the present case, it was not merely on the future likelihood of the interests of the general public being advanced that the impugned order was made by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The impugned order was made because, in the opinion of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	the presences of the petitioner was necessary for giving evidence before <ORG> and according to the report received by <ORG> she was likely to leave India and that might frustrate or impede to some extent the inquiries which were being conducted by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	[711-C-D] <NAME>, J.
RatioOfTheDecision	(concurring with <NAME>, J.)
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> has frowned on foreign travels by Indian patriotic suspects and instances from <ORG> may abound.
RatioOfTheDecision	In many countries the passport and visa system has been used as potent paper curtain to inhibit illustrious writers, outstanding sta tesmen, humanist churchmen and renowned scientists, if they are dissenters, from leaving their national frontiers.
RatioOfTheDecision	Things have changed, global awareness has dawned.
RatioOfTheDecision	The European Convention on Human Rights and bilateral understandings have made headway to widen freedom of travel abroad as integral to liberty of the person.
RatioOfTheDecision	And the universal Declaration of Human Rights has proclaimed in Article 13 , that every one has the right to leave any country including his own, and to return to his country.
RatioOfTheDecision	This human planet is our single home, though geographically variegated, culturally diverse, politically pluralist in science and technology competitive and co-operative in arts and life-styles a lovely mosaic and, above all, suffused with a cosmic unconsciousness of unity and inter- dependence.
RatioOfTheDecision	[717 B, C, D, E-F] Viewed from another angle, travel abroad is a cultural enrichment which enables one's understanding of one's own country in better light.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus it serves national interest to have its citizenry see other countr ies and judge one's country on a comparative scale.
RatioOfTheDecision	[718 B] The right of free movement is a vital element of personal liberty.
RatioOfTheDecision	The right of free movement includes right to travel abroad.
RatioOfTheDecision	Among the great guaranteed rights life and liberty are the first among equals, carrying a universal connotation cardinal to a decent human order and protected by constitutional armour.
RatioOfTheDecision	Truncate liberty in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	21 traumatically and the several other freedoms fade out automatically.
RatioOfTheDecision	[720 A-B] Personal liberty makes for the worth of the human person.
RatioOfTheDecision	Travel makes liberty worthwhile.
RatioOfTheDecision	life is a terrestrial opportunity for unfolding personality rising to a higher scale moving to fresh woods and reaching out to reality which makes our earthly journey a true fulfilment, not a tale told by an idiot full of sound and fury signifying nothing, but a fine frenzy rolling between heaven and earth.
RatioOfTheDecision	The spirit of Man is at the root of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	21 Absent liberty, other freedoms are frozen.
RatioOfTheDecision	[721 C-F] Procedure which deals with the modalities of reg ulating, restricting or even rejecting a fundamental right falling within Article 21 has to be fair, not foolish, carefully designed to effectuate, not to subvert, the substantive right itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus, understood, 'procedure' must rule out anything arbitrary, freakish or bizarre.
RatioOfTheDecision	What is fundamental is life and liberty.
RatioOfTheDecision	What is procedural is the manner of its exercise.
RatioOfTheDecision	This quality of fairness in the process is emphasised by the strong word "establish" which means 'settled firmly'," not wantonly or whimsically.
RatioOfTheDecision	[722 H, 723 A-B] Procedure in Article 21 means fair, not formal procedure.
RatioOfTheDecision	Law is reasonable law, not any enacted piece.
Precedent	As Art.
Precedent	22 specifically spells out the procedural safeguards for preventive and punitive detention, a law providing for such detention should conform to Art.
Precedent	22.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has been rightly pointed out that for other rights forming part of personal liberty, the procedural safeguards enshrined in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	21 are available.
RatioOfTheDecision	Otherwise, as the procedural safeguards contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	22 will be availab le only in cases of preventive and punitive detention the right to life, more fundamental than any other forming part of personal liberty and paramount to the happiness, dignity and worth of the individual, will not be entitled to any procedural safeguard, save such as a legislature's mood chooses.
RatioOfTheDecision	[723 F-H] <NAME>'s case (AIR 1960 SC 1080, 1093) referred.
RatioOfTheDecision	Liberty of locomotion into alien territory cannot be unjustly forbidden by the <ORG> and passport legislation must take processual provisions which accord with fair norms, free from extraneous pressure and, by and large, complying with natural justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	Unilateral arbitrariness, police dossiers, faceless affiants, behind- the-back materials oblique motives and the inscrutable face of an official sphinx do not fill the 'fairness,' bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	[726 D-E] Article 21 clubs life with liberty and when we interpret the colour and content of 'procedure established by law', we must be alive to the deadly peril of life being deprived without minimal processual just ice, legislative callousness despising hearing and fair opportunities of defence.
RatioOfTheDecision	[726 F] Sections 5 , 6 and 10 of the impugned legislation must be tested even under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	21 on canons of processual justice to the people outlined above.
RatioOfTheDecision	Hearing is obligatory-meaningful hearing, flexible and realistic, according to circumstances' but not ritualistic and wooden.
RatioOfTheDecision	In exceptional cases and emergency situations, interim measures may be taken, to avoid the mischief of the passportee becoming an.
RatioOfTheDecision	escapee before the hearing begins.
RatioOfTheDecision	"Bolt the stables after the horse has been stolen" is not a command of natural justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	But soon after the provisional seizure, a reasonable hearing must follow, to minimise procedural prejudice.
RatioOfTheDecision	And when a prompt final order is made against the applicant or passport holder the reasons must be disclosed to him almost invariably save in those dangerous cases, where irreparable injury will ensue to the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	A government which revels in secrecy in the field of people's liberty not only acts a gainst democratic decency but busies itself with its own burial.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is the writing on the wall if history were teacher, memory our mentor and decline of liberty not our unwitting endeavour.
RatioOfTheDecision	Public power must rarely hide its heart in an open society and system.
RatioOfTheDecision	[727 F-H] Article 14 has a pervasive processual potency and versatile quality, equalitarian in its soul and allergic to discriminatory diktats.
RatioOfTheDecision	Equality is the antithesis of arbitrariness.
RatioOfTheDecision	[728 A] As far as question of extra-territorial jurisdiction in foreign lands is concerned, it is a misconception.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nobody contends that India should interfere with other countries and their sovereignty to ensure free movement of Indians in those countries.
RatioOfTheDecision	What is meant is that <ORG> should not prevent by any sanctions it has over its citizens from moving within in any other country if that other country has no objection to their travelling within its territory.
Precedent	[728 C] In <NAME>'s case it was held that Art.
Precedent	22 is a self- contained Code, however,  'this has suffered supersession at.
Precedent	the hands of <NAME>.
Precedent	[728 D] <ORG>  3 SCR 842, <ORG>  3 SCR 530.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME>  2 SCR 759 and <NAME>  1 SCR 856 referred to.
RatioOfTheDecision	The law is now settled that no article in Part III is an island but Part of a continent, and the conspectus of the whole part gives the direction and correction needed for interpretation of these basic provisions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Man is not dissectible into separate limbs and, likewise, cardinal rights in an organic constitution, which make man human have a synthesis.
RatioOfTheDecision	The proposition is indubitable that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	21 does not, in a given situation exclude Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19 if both rights are breached.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is a salutary thought that the summit court should not interpret constitutional rights enshrined in Part III to choke its life-breath or chill its elan vital by processes of legalism, overruling the enduring values burning in the bosoms of those who won our independence and drew up our founding document.
RatioOfTheDecision	[728 F-G, 729 A-B] High constitutional policy h as harmonised individual freedoms with holistic community good by inscribing exceptions to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19(1) in Art 19(2) to (6).
RatioOfTheDecision	Even so, what is fundamental is the freedom, not the exception.
RatioOfTheDecision	More im- portantly, restraints are permissible only to the extent they have nexus with the approved object.
RatioOfTheDecision	No verbal labels but real values are the governing considerations in the exploration and adjudication of constitutional prescriptions and proscriptions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Governments come and go, but the fundamental rights of the people cannot be subject to the wishful value-sets of political regimes of the passing day.
RatioOfTheDecision	[729 C-D, 730 F] Locomotion in some situation is necessarily involved in the exercise of the specified fundamental rights as an associated or integrated right.
RatioOfTheDecision	Travel, simpliciter, is peripheral to and not necessarily fundamental in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19 Free speech is feasible without movement beyond country.
RatioOfTheDecision	[731 B] The delicate, yet difficult, phase of the controversy arrives where free speech and free practice of profession are ine xtricably interwoven with travel abroad.
RatioOfTheDecision	638  One, has to view the proximate and real consequence of thwarting transnational travel through the power of the <ORG> exercised under s. 3 of the Passport Act read with ss.
RatioOfTheDecision	5 and 6 .
RatioOfTheDecision	Associated rights totally integrated with fundamental rights must enjoy the same immunity.
RatioOfTheDecision	Three sets of cases might arise.
RatioOfTheDecision	First, where the legislative provision or executive' order expressly forbids exercise in foreign lands of the-fundamental right while granting passport.
RatioOfTheDecision	Secondly, there may be cases where even if the order is innocent on its face, the refusal of permission to go to a foreign country may, with certainty and immediacy, spell denial of free speech and professional practice or business.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thirdly, the fundamental right may itself enwomb locomotion regardless of national frontiers.
RatioOfTheDecision	The second and third often are blurred in their edges and may overlap.
RatioOfTheDecision	[732 H, 733 A-C] Spies, traitors, smugglers, saboteurs of the health, wealth and survival or sovereignty of the nation s hall not be passported into hostile soil to work their vicious plan fruitfully.
RatioOfTheDecision	But when applying the Passports Act , Over- breadth, hyper-anxiety, regimentation complex, and political mistrust shall not sub-consciously exaggerate, into morbid or neurotic refusal or unlimited imponding or final revocation of passport, facts which, objectively assessed, may prove tremendous trifles.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is why the provisions have to be read down into constitutionality, tailored to fit the reasonableness test and humanised by natural justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Act willsurvive but the order shall perish for reasons so fully set out by <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	And on this construction, the conscience of the Constitution triumphs over vagarious governmental orders.
Precedent	[734 E-G-H] <NAME>, J.
Precedent	(Dissenting) The preamble to the Constitution provides that the people of India have solemnly resolved to constitute India into a sovereign, socialist, secular and democratic republic and to secure to all its citizens, justice, social, economic and political, l iberty of thought, expression, belief, faith and worship, equality of status and of opportunity.
Precedent	Article 12 defines the <ORG> as including the <ORG> and <ORG> of India and the <ORG> and the <ORG> of each of the <ORG>s and of local or other authorities within the territory of India or under the control of the <ORG> of India.
Precedent	Article 13 provides that laws that are inconsistent with or in derogation of fundamental rights are to that ex- tent void.
Precedent	Article 245(2) provides that no law made by <ORG> shall be deemed to be invalid on the ground that it would have extra territorial operation.
Precedent	In England section 3 of the Statute of Westminster declares that <ORG> has full power to make laws having extra territorial operation.
Precedent	The following are the principles to determine whether the provisions of a Constitution or a Statute have extra territorial application.
Precedent	(a) An Act unless it provides otherwise applies only to the country concerned.
RatioOfTheDecision	(b) An Act of a <ORG> will bind the sub jects of the realm both within and without if that is the intention of the <ORG>, which must be gathered from the language of the Act in question.
RatioOfTheDecision	(c) <ORG> normally restricts operation of legislation to its own territories.
RatioOfTheDecision	However, on occasions legislation controlling the activities of its own citizens when they are abroad may be passed.
Precedent	<NAME> v. <NAME> 48 L.J.P.I.
Precedent	at p. 10 and <NAME> v. <NAME> and Others [1896] 2 Q.B.
Precedent	Division 425 at 430 referred to.
Precedent	(d) In the absence of an intention clearly expressed or to be inferred from its language, or from the object or subject matter or history of the enactment, the presumption is that <ORG> does not design its statute to operate beyond the territorial limit of the country.
Precedent	[738-E-F-H, 739 A, B, E, G-H, 740 A, B, G-H] Governor-General in Council v. <ORG> A.I.R.
Precedent	(31) [1944] <ORG> 51, referred to.
Precedent	<CITATION_114104>  F.C.R.
Precedent	65 and Mohammad Mohy-ud-din v. The King Emperor  F.C.R.
Precedent	94 referred to.
RatioOfTheDecision	639  The applicatio n of Article 14 is expressly limited to the territory of India Articles 15, 16, 17, 18, 20 and 22 by their very nature are confined to the territory of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	Articles 23 to 28 are applicable only to the territory of India At any rate, there is no intention in these Articles indicating extra-territorial application.
RatioOfTheDecision	So also Articles 29 and 30 which deal with cultural and educational rights are applicable only within the territory of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 31 does not expressly or impliedly have any extra- territorial application.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is possible that the right conferred by Article 19(1)(a) may have extra-territorial application.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not likely, however, that the framers of the Constitution intended the right to assemble peaceably and without arms or to form associations or unions or to acquire, hold and dispose of property, or to practise any profession or to carry on any occupation, trade or business, to have any extra-territorial application for such rights would not- be enforced by the <ORG> outside the Indian te rritory.
RatioOfTheDecision	The rights conferred under Article 19 are fundamental rights and Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	32 & 226 provide, that those rights are guaranteed and can be enforced by the aggrieved person by approaching this <ORG> or the High <ORG>s.
RatioOfTheDecision	These rights cannot be protected by the <ORG> outside its territory and, therefore, there is a presumption that the constitution makers would not have intended to guarantee any rights which the <ORG> cannot enforce.
RatioOfTheDecision	[742 H, 743 A-D-E-F] <NAME> v. The <ORG> of Punjab and Another,  SCR 308 referred to.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is most unlikely that before the declaration of human rights was promulgated the framers of the Constitution decided to declare that the fundamental rights conferred on the citizens would, be available even outside India.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even in the American Constitution there is no mention of right to freedom of speech or expressions as being available outside America.
RatioOfTheDecision	The law made under Article 19(2) to 19(6) imposes restrictions on the exercise of right of freedom of speech and expression etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	The restr ictions thus imposed normally would apply only within the territory of India unless the legislation expressly or by necessary implication provides for extra-territorial operation.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the penal code, section 3 and 4 specifically provides that crimes committed by citizens of India outside India are punishable.
RatioOfTheDecision	In Article 19 , however, there is no such provision expressly or by necessary implication.
RatioOfTheDecision	Secondly, a citizen cannot enforce his fundamental rights outside the territory of India even if it is taken that such rights are available outside the country.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, the contention of the petitioners that by denying the passport the petitioner's fundamental rights guaranteed by Article 19 are infringed cannot be accepted.
RatioOfTheDecision	[744 H, 745 A-D, 746 F-G, H, 747 A] The important question which arises, is whether an Act passed under Article 21 should also satisfy requirements of Article 19.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has been decided by <ORG> in <NAME>'s case that the punitive detention for offences under the Penal Code cannot be chall enged on the ground that it infringes fundamental rights under Article 19.
RatioOfTheDecision	[747 E-F] The rights guaranteed under Article 19(1) are subject to restrictions that may be placed by Articles 19(2) to 19(6).
RatioOfTheDecision	The right not to be deprived of life and personal liberty is subject to its deprivation by procedure established 'by law.
Precedent	In <NAME>'s case it was held that Article 19 dealt with the rights of the citizens when he was free and would not apply to person who had ceased to be free and has been either under punitive or preventive detention.
Precedent	It was further held that Article 19 only applied where a legislation directly hit the rights enumerated in the Article and not where the loss of rights mentioned .in the Article was a result of the operation of legislation relating to punitive or preventive detention.
Precedent	The aforesaid ratio of <NAME>'s case has been confirmed by this, Court in <CITATION_959860> .

Precedent	There can be no distinction between the punitive detention and preventive detention.
