Precedent	Article 15 relates to prohibition of discrimination on grounds of religion, race, caste, sex or place of birth, and Art.
Precedent	16 deals with equality of opportunity in matters of public employment.
Precedent	By their very nature the two Articles are confined to the territory of India.
Precedent	So also Articles 17 and 18 which deal with abolition of untouchability and abolition of titles.
Precedent	Before dealing with Articles 19 and 21 with which we are now concerned the other articles may be referred to in brief.
RatioOfTheDecision	Articles 20 and 22 can have only territorial application.
RatioOfTheDecision	Articles 23 and 24 which relate to right against exploitation and Articles 25 to 28 which relate to freedom of conscience and free profession, practice and propagation of religion etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	prima focie are applicable only to the territory of India At any rate there is no intention in these Articles indicating extra- territorial application.
RatioOfTheDecision	So also articles 29 and 30 which deal with cultural and educational rights are applicable only within the territory of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 31  does not expressly or impliedly have any extra territorial appli- cation.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this background it will have to be examined whether any express or implied intention of extra- territorial applicability is discernible in Articles 19 and  21.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 19(1) (a) declares the right to freedom of speech and expression.
RatioOfTheDecision	While it is possible that this right may have extra-territorial application, it is not likely that the framers of the Constitution intended the right to assemble peaceably and without arms or to form associations or unions, or to acquire hold and dispose of property, or to practise any profession, or to carry on any occupation, trade or business, to have any extra territorial application, for such rights could not be enforced by the <ORG> outside the Indian territory.
RatioOfTheDecision	The rights conferred under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19 are Fundamental Rights and Articles 32 and 226 provide that these rights are guaranteed and can be enforced by the aggrieved person by approaching <ORG> or <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Admittedly, the rig hts enumerated in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19(1) (a ), (b ), (c ), (f) and (g) cannot be enforced by the <ORG> and in the circumstances there is a presumption that the Constitution-makers would have intended to guarantee any right which the <ORG> cannot enforce and would have made a provision guaranteeing the rights and securing them by recourse to <ORG> and <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The restriction of the right to move freely throughout the territory of India and the right to reside and stay in any part of the territory of India is strongly relied upon as indicating that in the absence of such restrictions the other rights are not confined to the, territory of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	The provisions in Art.
Precedent	19 (1) (d) and (e) i.e.
Precedent	the right to move freely throughout the territory of India and to reside and settle in any part of the territory of India have historical significance.
Precedent	<CITATION_1857950> ,(1) <NAME>, said that in the right "to move freely throughout the territory of India" the emphasis was not on the free movement but on the ri ght to move freely throughout the territory of India.
Precedent	The intention was to avoid any restriction being placed by the States hampering free movement (1)  S.C.R.
Precedent	88.
Precedent	744  throughout the territory of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is a historical fact that there were rivalries between the various States and the imposition of restraint on movement from State to State by some States was not beyond possibility.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the two clauses 19 (1) (d) and (e) the right "to move freely throughout the territory of India" and "to reside and settle in any part of the territory of India" the "territory of India" is mentioned with the purpose of preventing the States from imposing any restraint.
RatioOfTheDecision	From the fact that the words "territory of India" are found in these two clauses the contention that the other freedoms are not limited to the territory of India for their operation cannot be accepted.
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_1475436> ,(1) <NAME>, C. J., who spoke on behalf of <ORG> stated : "The point to, be kept in view is that several rights of freedo m guaranteed to the citizens by Article 19(1) are exercisable by them throughout and in all parts of the territory of India".
RatioOfTheDecision	The view that the rights under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19 (1) is exercisable in the territory of India has not been discussed.
RatioOfTheDecision	Far from Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19(1) expressing any intention expressly or impliedly of extra territorial operation the context would indicate that its application is intended to be only territorial.
RatioOfTheDecision	The right under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19(b) and (c) to assemble peaceably and without arms and to form associations or unions could not have been intended to have any extraterritorial application as it will not be in accordance with the accepted principles of international law.
RatioOfTheDecision	As the rights under Articles 19(b) and (c) cannot be enforced outside India the inference, is that no extra- territorial application was intended.
Precedent	So also regarding the rights conferred under Articles 19(f) and (g) i.e.
Precedent	to acquire, hold and dispose of property; and to practise any profession, or to carry on any occupation, trade or business, w ould not have been intended to be applicable outside India.
Precedent	It was submitted that when the Constitution was framed the founding fathers were influenced by <ORG> which was made in December, 1948 and they thought it fit to make the Fundamental Rights available to the Indian citizens throughout the world.
RatioOfTheDecision	The history of the conception of hu- man rights may be shortly traced.
RatioOfTheDecision	The main task of <ORG> which was set up by <ORG> was to draw an International Bill of Rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> split this task into two documents : a short declaration of principles and an elaborate treaty or covenant enforcing those principles so far as practicable.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Universal Declaration of Human Rights was not intended to be binding as law but to present the main ideals of human rights and freedoms in order to inspire everybody, whether in or out of governments, to work for their progressive realization.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> finished the Declaration and it  was promulgated by <ORG> on December 10, 1948.
RatioOfTheDecision	The discussion about the Draft Indian Constitution took place between February and October, 1948 and the Articles relating to the Fundamental Rights were discussed in October, 1948, i.e.
RatioOfTheDecision	before the Universal Declaration of Human Rights was promulgated by <ORG> on December 10, 1948.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is most unlikely that before the Declaration of Human Rights was promulgated ' (1) [1958]S.C.R.308.
RatioOfTheDecision	745  the framers of the Indian Constitution decided to declare that the Fundamental Rights conferred on the citizens would have application even outside India.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Universal Declaration of Human Rights was not binding as law but was only a pious hope for achieving a common standard for all peoples and all nations.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 13 of the Declaration which is material for our discussion runs as follows : Paragraph 1.
Fact	Everyone has the right to freedom of movement and residence with in the borders of each state.
Precedent	Paragraph 2.
Precedent	Everyone has the right to leave any country, in cluding his own, and to return to his country.
Precedent	Paragraph 1 restricts the right of movement and residence specifically within the borders of the country.
Precedent	The second, paragraph aims at securing the right to leave any country including his own and to return to his country.
Precedent	The Declaration at that stage did not have any idea of conferring on the citizens of any country right of movement beyond borders of the <ORG> or to freedom of speech or right to assemble outside the country of origin.
Precedent	Even in the American Constitution there is no mention of right to freedom of speech or expression as being available outside America.
Precedent	Regarding the right of movement within the borders of the <ORG> it is not mentioned as one of the freedoms guaranteed in the American Constitution but everyone in the country takes it for granted that one can roam at will throughout the United <ORG>s.
Precedent	The right of a citizen to leave any country and to return to his country is recognised in the United <ORG>s.
Precedent	While there is no restriction on the c itizen to return to his own country <ORG> does place certain restrictions for leaving the country, such as obtaining of the passports etc.
Precedent	Even the right to travel outside the United States is not unrestricted.
Precedent	A passport is a request by the Government which grants it to a foreign Government that the bearer of the passport may pass safely and freely.
Precedent	The passport is considered as a licence for leaving a country and an exit permit rather than a letter of introduction.
Precedent	Even in America <ORG> when it issues a passport specifies that they are not valid for travel to countries in-which the United States have no diplomatic representation as the position of the Government is that it will not facilitate overseas travel where it is unable to afford any protection to the traveller.
Precedent	The American public particularly the news reporters are claiming that they should be allowed to travel wherever they wish if need be without their Government's assurance to protection.
RatioOfTheDecision	'The right o f the American citizen to travel abroad as narrated above shows that even the right to travel outside the country is not unfettered.
RatioOfTheDecision	In vain one looks to the American law to find whether the citizens are granted any right of freedom of speech and expression beyond the territory of the United States.
RatioOfTheDecision	The First Amendment provides for freedom of speech and press along with freedom of religion.
RatioOfTheDecision	Liberty of speech and liberty of press are substantially identical.
RatioOfTheDecision	They are freedom to utter words orally and freedom to write, print and circulate words.
RatioOfTheDecision	But this freedom of expression would be meaningless if people were not permitted to gather in groups to discuss mutual problems and communicate their feelings and opinions to governmental officers.
RatioOfTheDecision	The First Amendment therefore provides that the people have the right to assemble peaceably and petition the government for redress of grievances.
RatioOfTheDecision	The- petition for redress can only be confined to the United States of America.
Argument	In a recent address on Human Rights <NAME> <NAME>, U.S. Deputy Secretary of <ORG> reproduced in Shan, October 1977, stated before <ORG> in Chicago that the promotion of human rights has become a fundamental tenet of the foreign policy of <ORG>.
Argument	In explaining the conception of human rights and its practice in America the Deputy Secretary stated that the efforts should be directed to the most fundamental and important human rights all of which are internationally recognised in the Universal Declaration of Human Rights which <ORG> approved in 1948.
Argument	While emphasing the three categories of human rights (1) the right to be free from the governmental violation of the integrity of the person; (2)- the right to fulfilment of such vital needs as food, shelter, health care and education, and (3) the right to enjoy civil and political liberties, he stated that the freedom of thought, of religion, of assembly, of speech, of the press, freedom of movement within the outside one's own country; freedom to take p art in government, were liberties which American enjoy so fully, and too often take for granted, are under assault in many places.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be noted that while freedom of movement is referred to as both within and outside one's own country the other rights such as freedom thought, of religion, of assembly of speech, of press, are not stated to be available outside one's own country.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is thus seen that except the right to movement outside one's own country other rights are not available extra-territorially even in America.
RatioOfTheDecision	The fundamental rights, under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19(1) of the Constitution are subject to the restrictions that may be placed under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19(2) to (6) of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Fundamental Rights are not absolute but are subject to reasonable restrictions Provided for in the Constitution itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	The restrictions imposed are to be by operation of any existing law or making of a law by the <ORG> imposing reasonable restrictions.
RatioOfTheDecision	The scheme of the Article, thus it while conferring Fundamental Rights on  the citizens is to see that such exercise does not affect the rights of other persons or affect the society in general.
RatioOfTheDecision	The law made under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19(2) to (6), impose restrictions on the exercise of right of freedom of speech and expression, to assemble peaceably without arms etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	The restrictions thus imposed, normally would apply only within the territory of India unless the legislation expressly or by necessary implication provides for extra-territorial operation.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the Penal Code , under sections 3 and 4 , the Act is made specifically applicable to crimes that are committed outside India by citizen of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	Neither in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19 of the Constitution nor in any of the enactments restricting the rights under Art.
Precedent	19(2) is there any provision expressly or by necessary implication providing for extra-territorial application.
Precedent	A citizen cannot enforce his Fundamental Rights outside the territory of India even if it is taken that such rights are available outside the country.

Statute	<NAME> held :  "If there is a legislation directly attempting to control a citizen's freedom of speech or expression, or his right to assemble peaceably and without arms etc.

RatioOfTheDecision	(1) [1950] S.C.R.

RatioOfTheDecision	748  <NAME> though he dissented from the majority view regarding the application of Article 19 to punitive detention observed follows "The Indian Penal Code does not primar ily or .... necessarily impose restrictions on the freedom of movement and it is not correct to say that it is a law imposing restrictions on the right to move freely.
RatioOfTheDecision	Its primary object is to punish crime and not to restrict movement......
RatioOfTheDecision	But if it (the Punishment) consists in imprisonment there is a restriction on movement.
RatioOfTheDecision	This restraint is imposed not under a law imposing restrictions on movement but under a law defining crime and making it punishable.
Precedent	The punishment is correlated directly With the violation of some other person's right and not with the right of movement possessed by the offender himself.
Precedent	In my opinion, therefore, the Indian Penal Code does not come within the ambit of the words 'law' imposing restrictions on the right to move freely."
Precedent	The learned Judge, Justice <NAME>, took a different view regarding preventive detention on the basis that it did not admit of a trial but the order of detention rested on an apprehended and not actual danger.
Precedent	Regarding punitive detention, the decision  of a Bench of five Judges in <NAME> v. State of West Bengal,(1) expressed the same view.
Precedent	Chief Justice <NAME> observed :  "It is not possible to think that a person who is detained will yet be free to move or assemble or form association or unions or have the right to reside in any part of India or have the freedom of speech or expression.
Precedent	Suppose, a person is prosecuted of an offence of cheating and convicted after trial, it is not open to him to say that the imprisonment should be tested with reference to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19 for its reasonableness.
RatioOfTheDecision	A law which attracts Article 19 , therefore, must be such as is capable of being tested to be reasonable under clauses (2) to (5) of Article 19."
RatioOfTheDecision	In the case of punitive detention, it will be open to the accused to raise all defences that are open to him in law, such as that there have been no violation of any law in force.

RatioOfTheDecision	19 and also adopted the preventive  detention to prevent the greater evil by imperilling security, the safety of the <ORG> and the welfare of the nation, it is not possible to think that a person who is detained will yet be free to move or assemble or form associations etc.

Precedent	763  The passport Act provides for issue of passports and tra vel documents for regulating the departure from India of citizens of India and other persons.

RatioOfTheDecision	If it is meant as being applicable in every case however remote it may be where the citizen's rights under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19(1) are affected, punitive detention will not be valid.

RatioOfTheDecision	If a passport is refused according to procedure established by law, the plea that his other fundamental rights are denied cannot be  raised if they are not directly infringed.
RatioOfTheDecision	The decisions of <ORG> wherein the right of person to travel abroad has been dealt with may be noticed.
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_1747577> (1) the <ORG> held that though a passport was not required for leaving, for practical purposes no one can leave or enter into India without a passport.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, a passport is essential for leaving and entering India.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> held the right to travel is part of personal liberty and a person could not be deprived of it except according to the procedure laid down by law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The view taken by the majority was that the expression "personal liberty" in Article 21 only excludes the ingredients of liberty enshrined in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19 of the Constitution and the exression 'personal liberty' would take in the right to travel abroad.
RatioOfTheDecision	This right to travel abroad is not absolute and is liable to be restricted according to the procedure established by law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The decision has made it clear that "personal liberty" is 'not one of the rights secured under Artic le 19 and, therefore, liable to be restricted by the legislature according to the procedure established by law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The right of an American citizen to travel is recognised.
RatioOfTheDecision	In <NAME> v. Dulles,(2) the <ORG> observed that the right to travel is a part of the 'liberty' of which the citizen cannot be deprived without due process of law under the Fifth Amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	"The freedom of movement across the frontiers in either direction, and inside frontiers as well, as a part of our heritage, Travel abroad, like travel within the country...... may be as close to the heart of the individual as the choice of what he eats, or wears, or reads.
RatioOfTheDecision	Freedom of movement is basic in our scheme of values."
RatioOfTheDecision	In a subsequent decision--<NAME> v. Rusk(3) the <ORG> sustained against due process attacks the Government's refusal to issue passports for travel to Ouba because the refusal was grounded on foreign policy considerations affecting all citizens.
RatioOfTheDecision	"The requirements of due process are a function not only of the extent of the governmental res triction imposed, but also of the extent of the necessity for the restriction."

Statute	765  Section 5 of the Act provides for applying for passports or trave l documents etc.
Statute	and the procedure for passing orders thereon.
Statute	On receipt of an application under sub-section (2) <ORG> may issue a passport or a travel document with endorsement in respect of the foreign countries specified in the application or issue of a pass- port or travel document with endorsement in respect of some foreign countries and refuse to make an endorsement in respect of other countries or to refuse to issue a passport or travel document and to refuse to make on the passport or travel document any endorsement.
Statute	In the event of <ORG> refusing to make an endorsement as applied for or refusal to issue a passport or a travel document or refusal of endorsement, the authority is required to record in writing a brief statement of its reasons and furnish to that person, on demand, a copy thereof unless the authority for reasons specified in sub- section (3) refuses to furnish a copy.
Statute	Section 6 provides that the refusal to make an endorsement shall be on one or other gr ounds mentioned in sub-sections (2) to (6).
Statute	Section 8 provides that every passport shall be renewable for the same period for which the passport was originally issued unless the passport authority for reasons to be recorded in witing otherwise determines.
Statute	Section 10 is most important as the impounding of the passport ,of the petitioner was ordered under section 10(3)   (c) of the Act.
Statute	Section 10(1) enables the passport authority to vary or cancel the endorsement on a passport or travel document or may with the previous approval of <ORG>, vary or cancel the conditions subject to which a passport or travel document has been issued, and require- the holder of a passport or a travel document by notice in writing, to deliver up the passport or travel document to it within such time as may be specified in the notice.
Statute	Sub-section (2) enables the bolder of a passport or a travel document to vary or cancel the conditions of the passport.
Statute	Section 10(3) with which we are concerned runs as follows 10( 3).-The passport authority may impound or cause to be impounded or revoke a passport or travel document,-  (a) If the passport authority is satisfied that the holder of the passport or travel document is in wrongful possession of;  (b) If the passport or travel document was obtained by the suppression of material information or on the basis of wrong information provided by the holder of the passpot or travel document or any other person on his behalf;  (c) If the passport authority deems it necessary so to do in the interests of the sovereignity and integrity of India, the security of India, friendly relations of India with any foreign country, or in the interests of the general public;  766  (d) If the holder of the passpot or travel document has, at any time after the issue of the passort or travel document, been convicted by a court in India for any offence involving moral turpitude and sentenced in respect thereof to imprisonment for not less than two years;  (c) If proceedings in respect of an offence al leged to have been committed by the holder of the passport or travel document are pending before a criminal court in India;  (f) If any of the conditions of the passport or travel document has been contravened;  (g) If the holder of the passport or travel document has failed to comply with a notice under sub-section (1)requiring him to deliver up the same.
Statute	(b) If it is brought to the notice of <ORG> that a warrant or summons for the appearance or a warrant for the arrest, of the holder of the passport or travel document has been issued by a court under any law for the time being in force or if an order prohibiting the departure from India of the holder of the passport or other travel document has been made by any such court and <ORG> is satisfied that a warrant or summons has been so issued or an order has been so made."
Statute	Section 10(3) (c) enables <ORG> to impound or revoke a passport if <ORG> deems it 'necessary so to do in the interests of  the sovereignty and integrity of India, the security of India, friendly relations of India with any foreign country, or in the interests of the general public.
Statute	Section 10(5) requires <ORG> to record in writing a brief statement of the reasons for making an order under sub-section (1) or(3) and to furnish the holder of the passport on demand a copy of the same unless in any case <ORG> is of the opinion that it will 'not be in the interests of the sovereignty and integrity of India, the, security of India, friendly relations of India with any foreign country or in the interests of the general public to furnish such a copy.
Statute	Section 11 provides for an appeal by the aggrieved person against any order passed by <ORG> under several clauses mentioned in sub- section (1) of that section.
Statute	It is also provided that no appeal shall lie against any order passed by <ORG>.
Statute	Section 11(5) provided that in disposing of an appeal, the appellate authority shall  follow such procedure as may be prescribed and that no appeal shall be disposed of unless the appellant has been given a reasonable opportunity of representing his case.
Statute	Rue 14 of the Passport Rules, 1967 prescribes that the appellate authority may call for the records of the case from the authority who passed the order appealed against and after giving the appellant a reasonable opportunity of representing his case pass final orders.
Statute	To sum up under section 10(3) (c) if the passport authority deems it necessary so to do for reasons stated in the subsection, he may impound a passport.
Statute	He is required to record in writing a brief statement of 'the reasons for making such order and to furnish a copy of the order on demand unless in any case he thinks for reasons mentioned in sub-section (5) that a copy should not be.
Statute	furnished.
RatioOfTheDecision	Except against an order passed by <ORG> the aggrieved person has a right of appeal.
RatioOfTheDecision	The appellate authority is required to give a reasonable opportunity to the aggrieved  person of representing his case.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was submitted on behalf of the petitioner that on a reading of section 10(3) observance of rules of natural justice, namely the right to be heard, is implied and as the Government had failed to give an opportunity to the petitioner to explain her case the order is unsustainable.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the alternative it was submitted that if section 10(3)   (c) is construed as denying the petitioner an opportunity of being heard and by the provisions of section 11 a right of appeal against an order passed by <ORG> is denied the provisions will not be procedure as established by law under Article 21 and the relevant sections should be held ultra vires of the powers of the legislature.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was contended that the power conferred on the authority to im- pound a passport in the interests of general public is very vague and in the absence of proper guidance an order by the authority impounding the passport "in the interests of general public" without any explanation is not valid.
RatioOfTheDecision	Th e last ground may easily be disposed of.
RatioOfTheDecision	The words 'in the interests of general public' no doubt are of a wide connotation but the authority in construing the facts of the case should determine whether in the interests of public the passport will have to be impounded.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whether the reasons given have annexus to the interests of general public would depend upon the facts of each case.
RatioOfTheDecision	The plea that because of the vagueness of the words 'interests of the general public' in the order, the order itself is unsustainable, cannot be accepted.
RatioOfTheDecision	The submission that in the context the rule of natural justice, that is, the right to be beard has not been expressly or by necessary implication taken away deserves careful consideration.
Statute	Under Section 10(3) the passport authority is authorised to impound or revoke a passport on any of the grounds specified in clauses (a) to (h) of sub- section (3).
Statute	Sub- section 3(a) enables the authority to impound a passport if the bolder of the passport is in wrongful possession thereof.
Statute	Und er sub- section 3(b) the authority can impound a passport if it was obtained by the suppression of material information or on the basis of wrong information provided by the holder of the passport.
Statute	Under clause (d) a passport can be impounded if the holder bad been convicted by <ORG> for any offence involving moral turpitude and sentenced to imprisonment for not less than two years.
Statute	Under clause (e) the passport can be impounded where proceedings in respect of an offence alleged to have been committed by the holder of a passport is pending before a criminal court in India.
Statute	Clause (f) enables the authority to impound the passport if any of the conditions of the passport have been contravened.
Statute	Under clause (g) the passport authority can act if the holder of the passport had failed to comply with a notice under sub-section (1) requiring him to deliver up the same.
Statute	Under sub-clause (h) a passport may be impounded if it is brought to the notice of the passport authority that a warrant or summons for appe arance of the holder of the passport has been issued by any court or if there is an order prohibiting departure from India of the holder of the passport has been made by a court.
RatioOfTheDecision	It will be noticed that when action is contemplated under any of the clauses (a), (b), (d), (e), (f) and (h), it is presumed that the authority will give notice, for the passport authority cannot be satisfied under sub-clause (a) that the holder is in wrongful possession thereof or under clause (b) that he obtained the passport by suppression of material information.
RatioOfTheDecision	Similarly under clause  (d) whether a person has been convicted by a court in India for any offence involving moral turpitude and sentenced to imprisonment for not less than two years, can only be ascertained after hearing the holder of the passport.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under clause (e) the fact whether proceedings in respect of an offence alleged to have been committed by the holder of the passport are pending before a criminal court can only be determined after notice to him.
RatioOfTheDecision	Equally whet her a condition of passport has been contravened under sub-clause (f) or whether he has failed to comply with a notice under sub- section (1) can be ascertained only after hearing the holder of the passport.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under clause (h) also a hearing of the holder of the passport is presumed.
RatioOfTheDecision	Reading clause (c) in juxtaposition with other 'Sub-clauses, it will have to determined whether it was the intention of the legislature to deprive a right of hearing to the holder of the passport before it is impounded or revoked.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this connection, it cannot be denied that the legislature by making an express provision may deny a person the right to be heard.
RatioOfTheDecision	Rules of natural justice cannot be equated with the Fundamental Rights.
Precedent	As held by <ORG> in <CITATION_47629> ,(1) that "Rules of natural justice are not embodied rules nor can they be elevated to the position of Fundamental Rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	Their aim is to secure justice or to prevent miscarriage of justice.These rules can operate only in areas not covered by any law <NAME> dly made.
RatioOfTheDecision	They do not supplant the law but supplement it.
RatioOfTheDecision	If a statutory provision can be read consistently With the principles of natural justice, the courts should do 'so.
Precedent	But if a statutory provision either specifically or by necessary implication excludes the application of any rules of natural justice then the court cannot ignore the mandate of the legislature or the statutory authority and read into the concerned provision the principles of natural justice."
Precedent	So also the right to be heard cannot be presumed when in the circumstances of the case there is paramount need for secrecy or when a decision will have to be taken in emergency or when promptness of action is called for where delay would defeat the very purpose or where it is expected (1)  1 S.C.R.
Precedent	791.
Precedent	769  that the person affected would take an obstructive attitude.
Precedent	To a limited extent it may be necessary te rovoke or to impound a passport without notice if there is real apprehension that the holder of the passport may leave the country if he bec omes aware of any intention on the part of <ORG> or the <ORG> to revoke or impound the passport.
Precedent	But that by itself would not justify denial of an opportunity to the holder of the passport to state his case before a final order is passed.
Precedent	It cannot be disputed that the legislature has not by express provision excluded the right to be heard.
Precedent	When <ORG> takes action under section 10(5) he is required to record in writing a brief statement of reasons and furnish a copy to the holder of the passport on demand unless he for sufficient reasons considers it not desirable to furnish a copy.
Precedent	An order thus passed is subject to an appeal where an appellate authority is required to give a reasonable opportunity to the holder of the passport to put forward his case.
Precedent	When an appeal has to be disposed of after given for a specified period the revocation or impounding during the without hearing the aggrieved person.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further when a passport is given for a specified period the revocation  or impounding during the period when the passport is valid can only be done for some valid reason.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is a difference between an authority revoking or modifying an order already passed in favour of a person and initially refusing to grant a licence.
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_986468> ,(1) <ORG> held that "it would not be proper to equate an order revoking or modifying a licence with, a decision not to grant a licence."
RatioOfTheDecision	In <NAME> v. Secretary of <ORG>, <ORG>) Lord <NAME> observed that "If his permit (alien) is revoked before the time limit expires he ought, I think, to be given an opportunity of making representation; for he would have a legitimate expectation of 'being allowed to stay for the permitted time."
RatioOfTheDecision	Lord <NAME> extended the application of the rule of audi alteram partem even in the case of a foreign alien who had no right to enter the country.
RatioOfTheDecision	When a permit was granted and was subsequently sought to be revoked it has to be treated differently from that of refusing permission at the first  instance.
RatioOfTheDecision	As in the present case the passport which has been granted is sought to be impounded the normal presumption is that the action will not be taken without giving a opportunity to the holder of the passport.
RatioOfTheDecision	Section 10(3) in enumerating the several grounds on which the passport authority may impound a passport has used the words like 'if the authority is satisfied', "the authority deems it necessary to do so."
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> in Durav- appah V. Fernando(3) after referring to an earlier decision in <NAME> v. Jayasinghe(4) disagreed with the decision holding "As a general rule that words such as 'where it appears to .
RatioOfTheDecision	.
RatioOfTheDecision	.
RatioOfTheDecision	.'
RatioOfTheDecision	or 'if it appears to the satisfaction of..... or 'if the considers it expedient that.
RatioOfTheDecision	.
RatioOfTheDecision	.
RatioOfTheDecision	.'
RatioOfTheDecision	or 'if the .... is satisfied that.
RatioOfTheDecision	.
RatioOfTheDecision	.

Precedent	<ORG> in disagreeing with this approach observed (1)  2 S.C.R.
Precedent	807.
Precedent	(2) [1969] 2 Ch.

Precedent	(3) [1967] 2 A. C. 337, (4) [1 958] 59 N.L.R.
Precedent	457.
Precedent	770  that these various formulae are introductory of the matter to be considered and are given little guidance upon the question of audi alteram partem.
Precedent	The statute can make itself clear on this point and if it does cadit quaestio.
Precedent	If it does not then the principle laid down in <NAME> v. <ORG>) where <NAME>, J. stated "A long course of decision, beginning with Dr. <NAME>'s case, and ending with some very recent cases, establish, that although there are no positive words in the statute requiring that the party shall be heard, yet the justice of the common law will supply the omission of the legislature."
RatioOfTheDecision	In the circumstances, there is no material for coming to the conclusion that the right to be heard has been taken away expressly or by necessary implication by the statute.
RatioOfTheDecision	I may at this stage refer to the stand taken by the learned Attorney-General on this question.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to him "on a true construction, the rule audi alteram partenm is not excluded in ordinary ca ses and that the correct position is laid down by <ORG> in the case of <CITATION_1297032> .
Precedent	( 2 ) The view taken by <NAME>, J. is that the rule of audi alteram partem is not excluded in making an order under sec.
Precedent	10(3) (c) of the Act.
Precedent	But the Attorney General in making the concession submitted that the rule will not apply when special circumstances exist such as need for taking prompt action due to the urgency of the situation or where the grant of opportunity would defeat the very object for which the action of impounding is to be taken.
Precedent	This position is supported by the decision of <ORG> in <NAME> v. Knaggs,(3) wherein it was stated 'it must, however, be borne in mind that there may be special circumstances which would satisfy a Governor, acting in good faith, to take action even if be did not give an opportunity to the person affected to make any relevant statement, or to correct or controvert any relevant statement brought forward to his prejudice."
RatioOfTheDecision	This extraordinary step  can be taken by <ORG> for impounding or revoking a passport when he apprehends that the passport holder may leave the country and as such prompt action is essential.
RatioOfTheDecision	These observations would justify the authority to impound the passport without notice but before any final order is passed the rule of audi alteram partem would apply and the holder of the passport will have to be <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am satisfied that the petitioner's claim that she has a right to be heard before a final order under s. 10(3) (c) is passed is made out.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this view the question as to whether sec.
RatioOfTheDecision	10(3) (c) is ultra vires or not does not arise.
Argument	it was submitted on behalf of the state that an order under subclause 10(3) (c) is on the subjective satisfaction of <ORG> and that as the decision is purely 'administrative in character it cannot be questioned in a court of law except on very limited grounds.
Precedent	Though the courts had taken the view that the principle of natural justice is inapplicable to administrative  orders, there 'is a change in the judicial opinion subsequently.
Precedent	The frontier between judicial or (1) 1723 1 Str.
Precedent	557 ; Mod.
Precedent	Rep. 148.
Precedent	(2) 76 B.L.R.

Precedent	(3) [1918] A.
Precedent	C 557 quasi judicial determination on the one hand and an executive or administrative determination on the other has become blurred.
Precedent	The rigid view that principles of natural justice applied only to judicial and quasi judicial acts and not to administrative acts no longer holds the field.
Precedent	The views taken by the courts on this subject are not con- sistent.
Precedent	While earlier decisions were in favour of administrative convenience and efficiency at the expense of natural justice, the recent view is in favour of extending the application of natural justice and the duty to act fairly with a caution that the principle 'should not be extended to the extreme so as to affect adversely the administrative efficiency.
Precedent	In this connection it is useful to quote the oft-repeated ,observations of Lord Justice <NAME> in <NAME> v. Duke of Norfolk(1) "The req uirements of natural justice must depend on the circumstances of the case, the nature of the inquiry, the rules under which the tribunal is acting , the subject matter that is being dealt with, and so forth .... but, whatever standard is adopted, one essential is that the person concerned should have a reasonable opportunity of presenting his case."

Precedent	This <ORG> in <CITATION_1748256> (1) and <CITATION_443837> ,(2) has held that a limited judicial scrutiny of the impugned decision on the point of rational and reasonable nexus was open to a court of law.
Precedent	An order passed by an authority based on subjective satisfaction is liable to judicial scrutiny to a limited extent has been laid down in <CITATION_811010> .
Precedent	(3) wherein construing the provisions of s. 3(2)(e) of the Indian Electricity Act 9 of 1910 as amended by the U.P.
Precedent	Act 30 of 1961, where the language used is similar to s. 10(3) (c) of the Passport Act , this <ORG> held that when the Government exercises its power on the ground that it "deems  such supply necessary in public interest" if challenged, the Government must make out that exercise of the power was necessary in the public interest.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Court is not intended to sit in appeal over the satisfaction of the Government.
RatioOfTheDecision	If there is prima facie evidence on which a reasonable body of persons may hold that it is in the public interest to supply energy to consumers the, requirements of the statute are fulfilled.
RatioOfTheDecision	"In our judgment, the satisfaction of the Government that the supply is necessary in the public interest is in appropriate cases not excluded from judicial review."
RatioOfTheDecision	The decisions cited are clear authority for the proposition that the order passed under s. 10(3) (c) is subject to a limited judicial scrutiny.
RatioOfTheDecision	An order under s. 10(3) (c) though it is held to be an administrative order passed on the subjective satisfaction of the authority cannot escape judicial scrutiny.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Attorney General fairly conceded that an order under S. 10 (3) (c) is subject to a judicial scrutiny and that it can be  looked into by the court to the limited extent of satisfying itself whether the order passed has a rational and reasonable nexus to the interests of the general public.
Argument	It was next contended on behalf of the petitioner that the provisions of s. 10(5) of the Act which empowers <ORG> or the <ORG> to decline furnishing the holder of the passport a brief statement of the reasons for making an order if the authority is of the opinion that it will not be in the interest of sovereignty and integrity of India, security of India, friendly relations of India with any foreign country, or in the interests of the general public is unsustainable in law.
Argument	It was submitted that along with the right to refuse to furnish a copy of the order made by the <ORG>, as a right of appeal is denied against an order made by the Central <ORG>.
Argument	the provisions should be regarded as.
Argument	total denial or procedure and arbitrary.
Argument	In view of the construction which is placed on S. 10(3) (c) that the holder of the passport  is entitled to be heard before <ORG> deems it necessary to impound a passport, it cannot be said that there is total denial of procedure.
Precedent	The authority under s. 10(5) is bound to record in writing a brief statement of the reasons for making an order and furnish to the holder of the passport or travel docu-  (1) [1966] Sup.

RatioOfTheDecision	The grounds on which the authority may refuse to furnish the reasons are the same as provided in s. 10 (3)(c) for impounding a passport but the two powers are exercisable in totally different contexts.
Fact	Under sec.
Fact	10(3), the question that has to be considered is whether the passport has to be impound ed in the interests of sovereignty and integrity of India etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	or in the interests of general public.
Precedent	In passing an order under sec.
RatioOfTheDecision	10(5) it has to be considered whether in the interests of the sovereignty and integrity of India etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	or in the interests of general public, furnishing of a copy of the reasons for the order, should be declined.
RatioOfTheDecision	Though the same grounds are mentioned for impounding a passport as well as for refusing to furnish the reasons for making an order, it would not mean that when an order under s. 10(3) (c) is passed it would automatically apply to s. 10(5) and for the same reason the authority can decline to furnish the reasons for the order.
RatioOfTheDecision	S. 10(5) says that the authority shall furnish to the holder of the passport on demand a copy unless in any case the authority it of opinion that it will not be in the interests of sovereignty and integrity of India etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	The expression "unless in any case" would indicate that it is not in every case that the authority can decline to furnish reasons f or the order.
RatioOfTheDecision	There may be some, cases, and I feel that it can be only in very rare cases, that a copy containing the reasons for making such order can be refused.
RatioOfTheDecision	Though rare there may be some cases in which: it would be, expedient for the authority to decline to furnish a copy of the reasons for making such order.
RatioOfTheDecision	But that could only be an exception is indicated from the fact that the aggrieved person has a right of appeal under s. 1  1 which has to be decided after giving a reasonable opportunity of representing his case.
RatioOfTheDecision	A reasonable opportunity cannot Ordinarily be given without disclosing to that person the reasons for the order.
RatioOfTheDecision	In those rare ,cases in which a copy for the reasons of the order is declined by the passport authority and is not furnished during the hearing of the appeal, it would furnish sufficient justification for the courts to have a close look into the reasons for the order and satisfy itself whether it has been properly made.
RatioOfTheDecision	But I am unable to, say that a provision which empowers th e authority to decline to furnish reasons for making the order is not within the competence of the legislature.
RatioOfTheDecision	The learned counsel for the petitioner, with some justification, submitted that if no reasons we furnished by the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	and no appeal is provided against the order of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	it would virtually amount to denial of procedure established by law as contemplated under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	21 of the ,Constitution of India.

RatioOfTheDecision	is bound to give an opportunity to the holder of the passport before finally revoking or impounding it.

RatioOfTheDecision	itself passes an order it should be presumed that it would have made the order after careful scrutiny.

RatioOfTheDecision	The Attorney General assured us that all the grounds urged before us by the petitioner and the grounds that may be urged before the authority will be properly considered by the authority and appropriate orders passed.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the result, I hold that the petitioner is not entitled to any of the fundamental ri ghts enumerated-in Article 19 of the Constitution and that the Passport Act complies with the requirements of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	21 of the Constitution and is in accordance with the procedure established by law.
RatioOfTheDecision	I construe section 10(3)(c) as providing a right to the holder of the passport to be heard before the passport authority and that any order passed under section 10(3) is subject to a limited judicial scrutiny by <ORG> and <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	In view of the statement made by the learned Attorney General to which reference has already been made in judgment, I do not think it necessary to formally interfere with the impugned order.
RulingByPresentCourt	I accordingly dispose of the Writ Petition without passing any formal order.
RulingByPresentCourt	There will be no order as to costs.

RulingByPresentCourt	We, accordingly, dispose  of the Writ Petition without passing any formal order.
RulingByPresentCourt	The passport will remain in the custody of the Registrar of this Court until further orders.
RulingByPresentCourt	There will be no order as to costs.
